صحیح قول یہی ہے۔
Chapter of the prayer of the sick. It has been said stating the school of Imam Abu Hanifa that sitting without an excuse during supererogatory prayer is disliked, and in the previous issue, the commentator mentioned that according to Imam Sahib, sitting without an excuse is not disliked. So the reconciliation is that according to the statement of al-Mabsut, the saying of Imam Sahib is one of non-dislike, and one saying is of dislike. So in the previous issue the correct saying was mentioned, and in this issue the other saying was mentioned. The ruling of sitting to pray in a ship without an excuse: the statements of the jurists. Whoever prays in a ship sitting without an excuse, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, and standing is better. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is not permissible except with an excuse, because complete ability is possible, so it should not be abandoned. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is the proof that generally in it dizziness of the head occurs, and it is like realized. However, standing is better because it is farther from the shade of disagreement, and leaving (the ship) is better as much as possible because it is more calming for his heart. And the disagreement is regarding the unmoorred (ship), and the moored one is like the shore: that is correct. The majority: And whoever prays in a moving ship sitting without any illness, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, and standing is better. According to the two companions, it will not be permissible except with an excuse, because he has ability to stand, so it should not be abandoned. And the proof of Imam Sahib is that generally in the ship the head spins, and it is like realized. But standing is better because it is farther from the doubt of disagreement, and leaving the ship as much as possible is better because there is tranquility of heart. And the disagreement is regarding the unmoorred ship, and the moored ship is like the shore: that is correct. The commentator of al-Inayah said: The one who prays in a ship will either be unable to stand or not unable. If unable, then by consensus sitting to pray is permissible. And if not unable, then there are two situations. The ship will be stationary or moving. If the first, then by consensus sitting to pray is not permissible. If the second, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, sitting to pray without any illness is permissible, but standing is more complete. And the two companions said: sitting to pray without an excuse is not permissible. This is the school of Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, Imam Ahmad. The proof of the two companions is that he has ability to stand, and in the case of ability to stand, standing should not be abandoned. Therefore, standing should not be abandoned in this case either. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that from praying standing in a moving ship, generally dizziness of the head occurs, and the predominant is like realized. For example, sleeping on one's side is considered an invalidator of ablution, because in that state generally the limbs relax and wind exits. So the predominant is considered realized and the ruling of nullification of ablution is applied. Similarly, here the possibility of dizziness is considered realized, so it is said that this person is unable to stand, and when unable to stand, there is no harm in sitting to pray. However, according to Imam Sahib, standing is better because standing is farther from the doubt of disagreement, i.e., there is disagreement among scholars about sitting to pray, but in the case of standing, one is saved from the trouble of disagreement. The author of al-Hidayah says: If possible, leaving the ship for prayer is better, because it gives the most tranquility to the heart. But if leaving the ship is not possible, and he did not leave but prayed in the ship, it is still permissible. The author of al-Hidayah says: The disagreement about the permissibility or impermissibility of sitting to pray without an excuse is in a ship that is not moored but moving. And a ship that is moored at the shore is like the shore itself, i.e., just as sitting to pray on land at the shore without an excuse is not permissible, similarly in a moored ship, sitting to pray without an excuse is not permissible. This is the correct statement.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling of praying while sitting in a moving ship without a valid excuse. The base ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa is that it is permissible, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn), hold it impermissible except in case of inability to stand. The author first reconciles two conflicting reports from Imam Abu Hanifa about sitting in supererogatory prayer, concluding that his established position is that it is not disliked (makruh) per se, though another narration reports dislike. The core issue is the effect of the ship's motion: Imam Abu Hanifa argues that since dizziness (دوران رأس) is predominant (غالب) in a moving ship, it is treated as if actually realized, effectively making the person unable to stand. This is analogous to the ruling that sleeping on one side nullifies wudu because it is presumed that wind escaped. Thus, by istihsan (juristic preference), sitting is allowed, as the person is considered unable to stand. The Sahibayn counter that since the person can physically stand, he must stand because complete ability (التام مقدور عليه) exists. The author adds that standing is still afdal (better) according to all, as it avoids the disagreement, and if possible, leaving the ship altogether for prayer is best. The disagreement only applies to a moving ship; a moored ship is like land, so sitting without excuse is impermissible by consensus. The text also notes that this is the position of the other three Sunni schools (Maliki, Shafi‘i, Hanbali) who side with the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
دوران رأس (dawarān ra’s): Dizziness or spinning of the head, considered a predominant (غالب) condition in a moving ship.
الغالب كالمتحقق (al-ghālib ka al-muḥaqqaq): The predominant (ghālib) is treated like the realized (muḥaqqaq) in legal rulings
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; departing from qiyās for a stronger reason (here, the predominant dizziness).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is praying while sitting in a moving ship permissible without an excuse?
A) Because the ship's motion makes standing impossible
B) Because dizziness is predominant and treated as actual inability
C) Because sitting is always better in travel
D) Because the ship is like a house

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits sitting prayer in a moving ship without excuse due to the principle that the predominant dizziness is treated as actual inability, though standing is superior.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
10 min
Qada’ for Prayers Missed Due to Unconsciousness: Istihsān, Qiyās, and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilāf
Ruling on making up prayers lost through unconsciousness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making up prayers missed due to unconsciousness, explain the istihsān reasoning, distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf on counting method, and identify the cross-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness lasting more than five prayers exempts qada’ due to excessive hardship, but short unconsciousness requires qada’.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a crucial question: are missed prayers due to unconsciousness (ighmā’) to be made up? The Hanafi school uses istihsān (juristic preference) to set a threshold: five prayers or less require qada’; more does not. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including the contrast with strict qiyās, the comparison with insanity, and a subtle disagreement among the early Hanafi imams on how to count the number of missed prayers.

Guided Path
1
State the relied-upon ruling
The passage begins with the final position: if unconsciousness lasted for five prayers or less, qada’ is required; if more, it is not. Note that 'more' is defined as exceeding a day and night.
This is the muftā bihī.
2
Contrast with the qiyās
The author then presents what strict analogy would demand: since unconsciousness is like insanity, no qada’ at all. Understand why the school departs from this qiyās.
The key is istihsān.
3
Analyze the istihsān rationale
The istihsān is based on hardship. Short unconsciousness yields few missed prayers, no hardship; long yields many, causing hardship. The Sharī‘a removes hardship. Identify the threshold: more than a day and night.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Differentiate unconsciousness from sleep
The passage distinguishes sleep because its extension is rare, so it is treated as a short excuse (qada’ always required). Highlight the legal reasoning.
Sleep does not benefit from the exemption.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf
Imām Muhammad counts by prayer times (awqāt); the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad) count by hours (sā‘āt). Note that practically both yield the same result, and the view of the two is preferred due to the reported athar.
The ikhtilāf is scholastic; the fatwa is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پانچ یا پانچ سے کم نمازوں میں بے ہوشی طاری رہی تو قضاء ہے اور اس سے زیادہ میں نہیں
ومن اغمى عليه خمس صلوات او دونها قضى وان كان اكثر من ذلك لم يقض وهذا استحسانا والقياس - ان لاقضاء عليه اذا استوعب الاغماء وقت صلوة كامل لتحقق العجز فشبه الجنون وجه الاستحسان ان المدة اذا طالت كثرت الفوائت فيحرج فى الادا و اذا قصرت قلت فلا حرج والكثير ان تزيد على يوم وليلة لانه يدخل في حد التكرار والجنون كالاغماء كذا ذكره ابوسليمان بخلاف النوم لان امتداده نادر فيلحق بالقاصر ثم الزيادة تعتبر من حيث الاوقات عند محمد لان التكرار يتحقق به وعندهما من حيث الساعات وهو المأثور عن علي و ابن عمر والله اعلم بالصواب
ترجمہ: اور جس پر پانچ نمازوں تک یا اس سے کم بے ہوشی طاری ہوئی تو ان کی قضاء کرے اور اگر ان سے زیادہ تو قضاء نہ کرے اور یہ استحسان ہے اور قیاس یہ ہے کہ اس پر قضاء نہ ہو جب کہ اغماء نے ایک نماز کا پورا وقت گھیر لیا کیونکہ عجز متحقق ہو گیا پس اغماء جنون کے مشابہ ہو گیا۔ اور استحسان کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ مدت اغماء جب دراز ہو جائے گی تو قضاء نمازیں بہت ہو جائیں گی پس ان کی قضاء کرنے میں حرج پڑ جائے گا۔ اور مدت تھوڑی ہوگی تو قضائیں تھوڑی ہوں گی اس لئے حرج میں نہ پڑے گا۔ اور کثیر یہ ہے کہ قضائیں ایک دن رات سے بڑھ جائیں کیونکہ وہ تکرار کی حد میں داخل ہو جاتی ہیں اور جنون اغماء کے مانند ہے ایسا ہی ابوسلیمان نے ذکر کیا ہے۔ بخلاف نیند کے اس لئے کہ نیند کا اس قدر دراز ہونا نادر ہے تو نیند کو عذر قاصر کے ساتھ لاحق کیا جائے گا۔ پھر زیادتی اور کثرت امام محمد کے نزدیک اوقات سے شمار سے معتبر ہے کیونکہ تکرار اسی کے ساتھ متحقق ہوگا۔ اور شیخین کے نزدیک ساعات سے شمار ہے ۔ یہی حضرت علی اور حضرت ابن عمر رضی اللہ تعالی عنہما سے مروی ہے واللہ اعلم بالصواب
If he was unconscious for five prayers or less, he makes them up; if more than that, he does not make them up. And this is istihsan. And qiyas is that there is no qada' upon him if the unconsciousness encompasses the entire time of a complete prayer, because inability is realized, so it is like insanity. The face of istihsan is that when the period becomes long, the missed prayers become many, so hardship occurs in performing them; and when it is short, they are few, so there is no hardship. And 'much' is that it exceeds a day and night, because it enters the limit of repetition. And insanity is like unconsciousness. Thus Abu Sulayman mentioned it. Unlike sleep, because its prolongation is rare, so it is attached to the short excuse. Then the increase [in number] is considered in terms of times (awqāt) according to Muhammad, because repetition is realized by it. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is in terms of hours (sā‘āt). And this is reported from 'Ali and Ibn 'Umar. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on qada’ (making up) of prayers missed due to unconsciousness (ighmā’). The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is istihsān: if the unconsciousness lasted for five prayers or less, the missed prayers must be made up; if it lasted longer (i.e., for a period during which six or more prayers were missed, meaning it exceeded a day and night), then no qada’ is required. The author contrasts this with strict qiyās, which would exempt all missed prayers because unconsciousness is similar to insanity (junūn) – both involve complete loss of consciousness and are considered overwhelming disabilities. However, istihsān looks at the length: a short unconsciousness imposes little hardship (ḥaraj) in making up a few prayers, while a long one would cause excessive hardship. The criterion for 'much' is set as exceeding one day and night (i.e., more than five prayer times) because that enters the realm of repetition (takrār). A subtle intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf is mentioned: Imam Muhammad considers the count of prayers by number of prayer times (awqāt) – each of the five daily prayers constitutes a unit. Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the two) consider it by hours (sā‘āt) – meaning if unconsciousness lasts more than 24 hours, it is considered 'much'. The text notes that the latter is the transmitted view from ‘Alī and Ibn ‘Umar. Sleep is distinguished because prolonged sleep is rare, so it is treated as a short excuse, requiring qada’. The ruling applies only to fard prayers; missed sunan are not mentioned here.

Key Terms
اغماء (ighmā’): Unconsciousness, a state in which a person loses awareness and control, but not lunacy.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that departs from qiyās to avoid hardship in long unconsciousness.
حرج (ḥaraj): Hardship or difficulty that justifies a concession in the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many missed prayers from unconsciousness require qada’?
A) All missed prayers regardless of length
B) Only the first five prayers if unconsciousness lasted more than a day and night
C) No prayers require qada’ because unconsciousness is like insanity
D) Only the first prayer requires qada’

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between short unconsciousness (qada’ required) and long unconsciousness (qada’ only for the first five prayers) based on istihsān and the principle of avoiding hardship.
